     <p><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;params=40.443322_N_79.943583_W_type:edu"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">40&deg;26?36?N</span> <span class="longitude">79&deg;56?37?W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">? / ?</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">40.443322&deg;N 79.943583&deg;W</span><span style="display:none">? / <span class="geo">40.443322; -79.943583</span></span></span></a></span></span></span> <b>Carnegie Mellon University</b> (<b>Carnegie Mellon</b> or <b>CMU</b>; <span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?r/ 'ar' in 'bard'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?r</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/e?/ long 'a' in 'base'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">e?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'g' in 'guy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">i</span></a></span> <span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'m' in 'my'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">m</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ short 'e' in 'bed'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'l' in 'lie'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">l</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">?</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania" title="Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect">Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.</p> 
     <p>The university began as the Carnegie Technical Schools founded by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a> in 1900. In 1912, the school became the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institute_of_Technology" title="Carnegie Institute of Technology">Carnegie Institute of Technology</a> and began granting four-year degrees. In 1967, the Carnegie Institute of Technology merged with the <a href="/wiki/Mellon_Institute_of_Industrial_Research" title="Mellon Institute of Industrial Research">Mellon Institute of Industrial Research</a> to form Carnegie Mellon University. The university's 140-acre (0.57&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) main campus is 3 miles (4.8&nbsp;km) from <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania" title="Downtown Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect">Downtown Pittsburgh</a> and abuts the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Museums_of_Pittsburgh" title="Carnegie Museums of Pittsburgh">Carnegie Museums of Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schenley_Park" title="Schenley Park">Schenley Park</a>, and the campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a> in the city's <a href="/wiki/Oakland_(Pittsburgh)" title="Oakland (Pittsburgh)">Oakland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel_Hill_(Pittsburgh)" title="Squirrel Hill (Pittsburgh)">Squirrel Hill</a> neighborhoods, partially extending into <a href="/wiki/Shadyside_(Pittsburgh)" title="Shadyside (Pittsburgh)">Shadyside</a>.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon has seven colleges and independent schools: the Carnegie Institute of Technology (engineering), <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_College_of_Fine_Arts" title="Carnegie Mellon College of Fine Arts">College of Fine Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_College_of_Humanities_and_Social_Sciences" title="Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences">Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mellon_College_of_Science" title="Mellon College of Science">Mellon College of Science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinz_College" title="Heinz College">H. John Heinz III College</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Computer_Science" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Computer Science">School of Computer Science</a>.</p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Carnegie,_three-quarter_length_portrait,_seated,_facing_slightly_left,_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg/200px-Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg" width="200" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg/300px-Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg/400px-Andrew_Carnegie%2C_three-quarter_length_portrait%2C_seated%2C_facing_slightly_left%2C_1913.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Carnegie,_three-quarter_length_portrait,_seated,_facing_slightly_left,_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <center>
         <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a>, founder of the Carnegie Technical Schools
        </center> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Post-<a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> industrialists accumulated unprecedented wealth and some were eager to found institutions in their names as part of philanthropy campaigns using portions of their vast wealth. <a href="/wiki/Washington_Duke" title="Washington Duke">Washington Duke</a> at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Cornell" title="Ezra Cornell">Ezra Cornell</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins" title="Johns Hopkins">Johns Hopkins</a> at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leland_Stanford" title="Leland Stanford">Leland Stanford</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Vanderbilt" title="Cornelius Vanderbilt">Cornelius Vanderbilt</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">Vanderbilt University</a> are several notable examples of Andrew Carnegie's <a href="/wiki/The_Gospel_of_Wealth" title="The Gospel of Wealth">gospel of wealth</a> mentality and Carnegie Mellon University is one such result.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon predecessor institution, Carnegie Technical Schools, was founded in 1900 in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_American" title="Scottish American">Scottish American</a> industrialist and philanthropist Andrew Carnegie, who wrote the time-honored words &quot;My heart is in the work&quot;, when he donated the funds to create the institution. Carnegie's vision was to open a vocational training school for the sons and daughters of working-class Pittsburghers (Many of whom worked in his mills). Carnegie was inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Pratt_Institute" title="Pratt Institute">Pratt Institute</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn,_New_York" title="Brooklyn, New York" class="mw-redirect">Brooklyn, New York</a> founded by industrialist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Pratt" title="Charles Pratt">Charles Pratt</a> in 1887, modeling many aspects for his own school from Pratt.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The campus began to take shape in the <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts architecture</a> style of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hornbostel" title="Henry Hornbostel">Henry Hornbostel</a>, winner of the 1904 competition to design the original institution and later the founder of what is now the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Architecture" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Architecture">Carnegie Mellon School of Architecture</a>. The name was changed to the Carnegie Institute of Technology in 1912, and the school began offering four-year degrees. In 1965, it merged with <a href="/wiki/Andrew_W._Mellon" title="Andrew W. Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>'s Mellon Institute of Industrial Research to become Carnegie Mellon University. In addition, Carnegie founded Carnegie Mellon's coordinate <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_colleges_in_the_United_States" title="Women's colleges in the United States">women's college</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Morrison_Carnegie_College" title="Margaret Morrison Carnegie College">Margaret Morrison Carnegie College</a> in 1903 (which closed in 1973).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:AWMellon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/200px-AWMellon.jpg" width="200" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/300px-AWMellon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/400px-AWMellon.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:AWMellon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <center>
         <a href="/wiki/Andrew_W._Mellon" title="Andrew W. Mellon">Andrew W. Mellon</a>, founder of the Mellon Institute
        </center> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>There was little change to the campus between the first and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">second World War</a>. A 1938 master plan by Githens and Keally suggested acquisition of new land along Forbes Avenue, but the plan was not fully implemented. The period starting with the construction of the Graduate School of Industrial Administration building (1952) and ending with Wean Hall (1971) saw the institutional change from Carnegie Institute of Technology to Carnegie Mellon University. New facilities were needed to respond to the University's growing national reputation in <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, business, robotics and the arts. In addition, an expanding student population resulted in a need for improved facilities for student life, athletics and libraries. The campus finally expanded to <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Avenue" title="Forbes Avenue">Forbes Avenue</a> from its original land along <a href="/wiki/Schenley_Park" title="Schenley Park">Schenley Park</a>. A ravine long known as &quot;the cut&quot; was gradually filled in to campus level, joining &quot;the Mall&quot; as a major campus open space.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg/220px-CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg/330px-CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg/440px-CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:CMU_Hamerschlag_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Hamerschlag and Roberts Halls are two of the teaching facilities of the 
        <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institute_of_Technology" title="Carnegie Institute of Technology">College of Engineering</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The buildings of this era reflect current attitudes toward architectural style. The <a href="/wiki/International_style_(architecture)" title="International style (architecture)" class="mw-redirect">International Style</a>, with its rejection of historical tradition and its emphases on functionalism and expression of structure, had been in vogue in urban settings since the 1930s. It came late to the Carnegie campus because of the hiatus in building activity and a general reluctance among all institutions of higher education to abandon historical styles. By the 1960s, it was seen as a way to accomplish the needed expansion and at the same time give the campus a new image. Each building was a unique architectural statement that may have acknowledged the existing campus in its placement, but not in its form or materials.</p> 
     <p>During the 1970s and 1980s, the tenure of University President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cyert" title="Richard Cyert">Richard Cyert</a> (1972?1990) witnessed a period of unparalleled growth and development. The research budget soared from roughly US$12 million annually in the early 1970s to more than US$110 million in the late 1980s. The work of researchers in new fields like <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> helped the university build on its reputation for innovation and practical problem solving. President Cyert stressed strategic planning and comparative advantage, pursuing opportunities in areas where Carnegie Mellon could outdistance its competitors. One example of this approach was the introduction of the university's &quot;<a href="/wiki/Andrew_Project" title="Andrew Project">Andrew</a>&quot; computing network in the mid-1980s. This pioneering project, which linked all computers and workstations on campus, set the standard for educational computing and established Carnegie Mellon as a leader in the use of technology in education and research. On April 24, 1984, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cmu.edu">cmu.edu</a>, Carnegie Mellon's Internet domain became among the first six <a href="/wiki/.edu" title=".edu">.edu</a> <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL" class="mw-redirect">URLs</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present">Present</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Present">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Wean_hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Wean_hall.jpg/220px-Wean_hall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Wean_hall.jpg/330px-Wean_hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Wean_hall.jpg/440px-Wean_hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Wean_hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Wean Hall, home of the world's first internet-enabled soda vending machine.
        <sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the 1990s and into the 2000s, Carnegie Mellon solidified its status among American universities, consistently ranking in the top 25 in the national <i><a href="/wiki/US_News_and_World_Report" title="US News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">US News and World Report</a></i> rankings, and in the top 50 amongst universities worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-topuniversities.com_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topuniversities.com-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon is distinct in its interdisciplinary approach to research and education. Through the establishment of programs and centers that are outside the limitations of departments or colleges, the university has established leadership in fields such as <a href="/wiki/Computational_finance" title="Computational finance">computational finance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_systems" title="Information systems">information systems</a> and management, arts management, product design, <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_economics" title="Behavioral economics">behavioral economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human-computer_interaction" title="Human-computer interaction" class="mw-redirect">human-computer interaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_technology" title="Entertainment technology">entertainment technology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Decision_science" title="Decision science" class="mw-redirect">decision science</a>. Within the past two decades, the university has built a new university center, theater and drama building (Purnell Center), business school building (Posner Hall), student union and several dormitories. Baker Hall was renovated in the early 2000s (decade), and new chemistry labs were established in Doherty Hall soon after. Several computer science buildings, such as <a href="/wiki/Newell_Simon_Hall" title="Newell Simon Hall">Newell Simon Hall</a>, also were established, renovated or renamed in the early 2000s (decade). The university has most recently completed building the Gates Hillman Complex and continues renovating historic academic and residence halls.</p> 
     <p>The Gates Hillman Complex, opened for occupancy on August 11, 2009, sits on a 5.6-acre (23,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) site on the university's West Campus, surrounded by Cyert Hall, the Purnell Center for the Arts, Doherty Hall, Newell-Simon Hall, Smith Hall, <a href="/wiki/Main_Building,_U.S._Bureau_of_Mines" title="Main Building, U.S. Bureau of Mines">Hamburg Hall</a> and the Robert Mehrabian Collaborative Innovation Center. It contains 318 offices as well as labs, <a href="/wiki/Computer_cluster" title="Computer cluster">computer clusters</a>, lecture halls, classrooms and a 250-seat auditorium. The Gates Hillman Complex was made possible by a $20 million lead gift from the <a href="/wiki/Bill_%26_Melinda_Gates_Foundation" title="Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Foundation">Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Foundation</a> and an additional $10 million grant from The Henry L. Hillman Foundation. The Gates Hillman Complex and the Purnell Center for the Arts are connected by the <a href="/wiki/Randy_Pausch" title="Randy Pausch">Randy Pausch</a> Memorial Footbridge.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On April 15, 1997, <a href="/wiki/Jared_Cohon" title="Jared Cohon">Jared L. Cohon</a>, former dean of the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Forestry_%26_Environmental_Studies" title="Yale School of Forestry &amp; Environmental Studies">Yale School of Forestry &amp; Environmental Studies</a>, was elected president by Carnegie Mellon's Board of Trustees. During Cohon's presidency, Carnegie Mellon has continued its trajectory of innovation and growth. He leads a strategic plan that aims to leverage the University's strengths to benefit society in the areas of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life_sciences" title="Life sciences">life sciences</a>, information and security technology, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a> and practices, the <a href="/wiki/Fine_arts" title="Fine arts" class="mw-redirect">fine arts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, and business and <a href="/wiki/Policy" title="Policy">public policy</a>. President Cohon term ended on June 30, 2013, after which returned to the faculty at Carnegie Mellon.</p> 
     <p>On July 1, 2003, Carnegie Mellon launched Insp!re Innovation, a $1 billion comprehensive fundraising campaign. Half of the campaign goal is intended for the endowment to provide long-lasting support for faculty, students and breakthrough innovations. As of June 1, 2013, the campaign had brought in $1.17 billion, with $570.9 million going toward Carnegie Mellon's endowment. It also enabled the university to establish 30 endowed professorships, 94 endowed fellowships and 230 endowed scholarships.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 7, 2011, <a href="/wiki/William_S._Dietrich_II" title="William S. Dietrich II">William S. Dietrich II</a>, the former chairman of Dietrich Industries, Inc., a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Worthington_Industries" title="Worthington Industries">Worthington Industries, Inc.</a>, pledged a gift of $265 million, effective on October 6, 2011, upon his death. In response to this gift, Carnegie Mellon renamed the College of Humanities of Social Sciences as the Marianna Brown Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences after William Dietrich's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>New York's Mayor <a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Bloomberg" title="Michael R. Bloomberg" class="mw-redirect">Michael R. Bloomberg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University's</a> President <a href="/wiki/John_Sexton" title="John Sexton">John Sexton</a> on April 23, 2012, announced an historic agreement between New York City, New York's MTA, and a consortium of world-class academic institutions, and private technology companies, that will lead to the creation in New York of a <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Urban_Science_and_Progress" title="Center for Urban Science and Progress">Center for Urban Science and Progress</a> (CUSP). The Center for Urban Science and Progress (CUSP) is an applied science research institute which will be a partnership of top institutions from around the globe, led by NYU and <a href="/wiki/NYU-Poly" title="NYU-Poly" class="mw-redirect">NYU-Poly</a> with a consortium of world-class universities including: The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Warwick" title="University of Warwick">University of Warwick</a>, Carnegie Mellon University, the <a href="/wiki/City_University_of_New_York" title="City University of New York">City University of New York</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Institute_of_Technology_Bombay" title="Indian Institute of Technology Bombay">Indian Institute of Technology Bombay</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a>.</p> 
     <p>In September 2012, Carnegie Mellon announced the construction of the Sherman and Joyce Bowie Scott Hall on the Pittsburgh campus. The new building will be situated between Hamerschlag Hall, Roberts Hall, and Wean Hall and will house the university-wide Wilton E. Scott Institute for Energy Innovation, a new nanotechnology research center, and the Biomedical Engineering Department.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>On February 5, 2013 Carnegie Mellon announced the selection of <a href="/wiki/Subra_Suresh" title="Subra Suresh">Subra Suresh</a>, Director of the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> and Dean of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_School_of_Engineering" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology School of Engineering">Massachusetts Institute of Technology School of Engineering</a>, as its ninth president effective July 1, 2013<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Peer institutions of Carnegie Mellon's institutional research and analysis program include <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">Georgia Tech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute">RPI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">Penn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis" title="Washington University in St. Louis">Washington University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings_and_reputation">Rankings and reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rankings and reputation">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em">

       <tr>

       </tr>

       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>49</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_THES_W_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_THES_W-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>22</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>Internationally, the QS World University Rankings ranks CMU 49th among the top universities in the world in the most current (2012?2013) world university rankings and 20th in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-topuniversities.com_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topuniversities.com-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> CMU placed 51st among the top universities in the world in the current Academic Ranking of World Universities (ARWU).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Times Higher Education ranks CMU 22nd among the premier universities worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Nationally, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a> ranks Carnegie Mellon 23rd among American research universities <sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> It was named one of the &quot;New <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivies</a>&quot; by <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon is ranked 1st for graduate studies in computer science, a position consistently held in the past except in 2009. It is also 5th for graduate studies in engineering, 7th for graduate studies in fine arts, 7th for graduate studies in electrical engineering, 9th for graduate studies in public affairs, 16th for graduate studies in business, 19th for graduate studies in economics, 9th for graduate studies in statistics, and 21st for graduate studies in psychology in the 2014 rankings released by US News and World Report.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews.com_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews.com-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> The undergraduate business program is also ranked 2nd for information systems, production/operations, and quantitative analysis, 5th for supply chain management, 9th for finance, 16th for entrepreneurship, 24th for general management, and 7th as an undergraduate business program overall by the 2011 US News and World Report.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews.com_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews.com-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2010, the <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal" class="mw-redirect">Wall Street Journal</a></i> ranked Carnegie Mellon 1st in computer science, 4th in finance, 7th in economics, 10th overall, and 21st in engineering according to job recruiters.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is one of 62 elected members of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>. <a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" title="BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect">BusinessWeek</a>'s rankings of college return on investment placed Carnegie Mellon at 17th in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon University ranks tenth among &quot;Best Engineering Colleges By Salary Potential&quot; in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" title="BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect">BusinessWeek</a>'s 2011 rankings of the best undergraduate business schools have also placed the <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a>'s undergraduate business program at 3rd for starting salaries and 4th for academic quality.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In SmartMoney's rankings of the most valuable colleges, Carnegie Mellon ranked 4th amongst private universities and 23rd amongst all universities nation-wide.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon is also a member of the Global University Leaders Forum of the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>, one of 26 institutions in the world and one of only 12 in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Reporter" title="The Hollywood Reporter">The Hollywood Reporter</a> ranked the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Drama" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Drama">School of Drama</a> number 4 in the world amongst drama schools.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon's 140-acre (0.57&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) main campus is three miles (5&nbsp;km) from downtown <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Schenley_Park" title="Schenley Park">Schenley Park</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Squirrel_Hill" title="Squirrel Hill" class="mw-redirect">Squirrel Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shadyside_(Pittsburgh)" title="Shadyside (Pittsburgh)">Shadyside</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oakland_(Pittsburgh)" title="Oakland (Pittsburgh)">Oakland</a> neighborhoods. Carnegie Mellon is bordered to the west by the campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>. Carnegie Mellon owns 81 buildings in the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_(Pittsburgh)" title="Oakland (Pittsburgh)">Oakland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel_Hill" title="Squirrel Hill" class="mw-redirect">Squirrel Hill</a> neighborhoods of Pittsburgh.</p> 
     <p>For decades the center of student life on campus was &quot;<i>Skibo Hall</i>&quot;, the University's student union. Built in the 1950s, Skibo Hall's design was typical of <a href="/wiki/Mid-Century_Modern" title="Mid-Century Modern" class="mw-redirect">Mid-Century Modern</a> architecture, but was poorly equipped to deal with advances in computer and internet connectivity. The original Skibo was razed in the summer of 1994 and replaced by a new student union that is fully wi-fi enabled. Known as <i>University Center</i>, the building was dedicated in 1996.</p> 
     <p>A large grassy area known as &quot;the Cut&quot; forms the backbone of the campus, with a separate grassy area known as &quot;the Mall&quot; running perpendicular. The Cut was formed by filling in a ravine (hence the name) with soil from a nearby hill that was leveled to build the College of Fine Arts building.</p> 
     <p>The northwestern part of the campus (home to Hamburg Hall, Newell-Simon Hall, Smith Hall, and Gates Hillman Complex) was acquired from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bureau_of_Mines" title="United States Bureau of Mines">United States Bureau of Mines</a> in the 1980s.</p> 
     <p>In 2006, Carnegie Mellon Trustee Jill Gansman Kraus donated the 80-foot (24&nbsp;m)-tall sculpture <a href="/wiki/Walking_to_the_Sky" title="Walking to the Sky">Walking to the Sky</a>, which was placed the lawn facing Forbes Ave between the University Center and Warner Hall. The sculpture was controversial for its placement, the general lack of input that the campus community had, and its aesthetic appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-thorn_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thorn-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:1508px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner"> 
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cmu_panorama.jpg" class="image" title="A panoramic view of Carnegie Mellon University's Pittsburgh campus from the College of Fine Arts Lawn.From left to right: College of Fine Arts, Hunt Library, Baker and Porter Hall, Hamerschlag Hall, University of Pittsburgh's Cathedral of Learning (in the background), Wean Hall and Doherty Hall, Purnell Center, and the University Center. Also visible are &quot;The Fence,&quot; and the &quot;Walking to the Sky&quot; sculpture."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cmu_panorama.jpg/1500px-Cmu_panorama.jpg" width="1500" height="163" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cmu_panorama.jpg/2250px-Cmu_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Cmu_panorama.jpg/3000px-Cmu_panorama.jpg 2x" /></a>
       </div> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cmu_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" /></a>

Also visible are &quot;The Fence,&quot; and the &quot;Walking to the Sky&quot; sculpture.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_Pittsburgh">Beyond Pittsburgh</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Beyond Pittsburgh">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In addition to its Pittsburgh campus, Carnegie Mellon has a branch campus in the Middle East, <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University_in_Qatar" title="Carnegie Mellon University in Qatar">Carnegie Mellon University in Qatar</a>, which offers a full undergraduate curriculum with degree programs in computer science, business administration, biology, computational biology, and information systems. It also has graduate-level extension campuses in <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_Silicon_Valley" title="Carnegie Mellon Silicon Valley">Mountain View, California</a> in the heart of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a> (offering masters programs in <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">Software Engineering</a> and Software Management). The <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a> maintains a satellite center in downtown <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> and the Heinz College maintains one in <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, Australia and one in Washington, DC. Carnegie Mellon also maintains the Carnegie Mellon Los Angeles Center in <a href="/wiki/North_Hollywood,_California" title="North Hollywood, California" class="mw-redirect">North Hollywood, California</a> where students in the Master of Entertainment Industry Management program are required to relocate to Los Angeles in their second year and attend classes at this facility. Carnegie Mellon's <a href="/wiki/Information_Networking_Institute" title="Information Networking Institute">Information Networking Institute</a> offers graduate programs in <a href="/wiki/Athens,_Greece" title="Athens, Greece" class="mw-redirect">Athens, Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kobe,_Japan" title="Kobe, Japan" class="mw-redirect">Kobe, Japan</a>, in collaboration with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Athens_Information_Technology&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Athens Information Technology (page does not exist)">Athens Information Technology</a> and the Hyogo Institute of Information Education Foundation, respectively. In the fall of 2007, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Aveiro,_Portugal" title="Aveiro, Portugal">Aveiro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lisbon,_Portugal" title="Lisbon, Portugal" class="mw-redirect">Lisbon, Portugal</a> were added to the <a href="/wiki/Information_Networking_Institute" title="Information Networking Institute">Information Networking Institute</a>'s remote locations. The Institute for Software Research International (ISRI) offers graduate programs in <a href="/wiki/Coimbra,_Portugal" title="Coimbra, Portugal" class="mw-redirect">Coimbra</a>, Portugal.The Entertainment Technology Center offers graduate programs in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, Japan, and Singapore. The <a href="/wiki/Human-Computer_Interaction_Institute" title="Human-Computer Interaction Institute">Human-Computer Interaction Institute</a> offers a masters degree in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Madeira" title="University of Madeira">University of Madeira</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>. The College of Engineering has an international location in <a href="/wiki/Kigali,_Rwanda" title="Kigali, Rwanda" class="mw-redirect">Kigali, Rwanda</a> offering the Master of Science in Information Technology.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_media.2C_entertainment.2C_and_culture">In media, entertainment, and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: In media, entertainment, and culture">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The Carnegie Mellon University campus in Pittsburgh has served as the locale for many motion pictures. Alumnus <a href="/wiki/George_A._Romero" title="George A. Romero">George A. Romero</a> filmed <i><a href="/wiki/Creepshow" title="Creepshow">Creepshow</a></i> (1982) in and around Margaret Morrison Carnegie Hall. Much of the on-campus scenes in the 2000 film <i><a href="/wiki/Wonder_Boys_(film)" title="Wonder Boys (film)">Wonder Boys</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Michael_Douglas" title="Michael Douglas">Michael Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tobey_Maguire" title="Tobey Maguire">Tobey Maguire</a>, were filmed in Carnegie Mellon's campus. Other movies filmed at Carnegie Mellon include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mothman_Prophecies_(film)" title="The Mothman Prophecies (film)">The Mothman Prophecies</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dogma_(film)" title="Dogma (film)">Dogma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lorenzo%27s_Oil_(film)" title="Lorenzo's Oil (film)" class="mw-redirect">Lorenzo's Oil</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Rises" title="The Dark Knight Rises">The Dark Knight Rises</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Flashdance" title="Flashdance">Flashdance</a></i>. The university is also featured prominently in the films <i><a href="/wiki/Smart_People" title="Smart People">Smart People</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Jessica_Parker" title="Sarah Jessica Parker">Sarah Jessica Parker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Quaid" title="Dennis Quaid">Dennis Quaid</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Monkey_Shines" title="Monkey Shines">Monkey Shines</a></i>, and in the anime <i><a href="/wiki/Summer_Wars" title="Summer Wars">Summer Wars</a></i>. It was also referenced on <a href="/wiki/Take_My_Life,_Please" title="Take My Life, Please">an episode</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Weeds_(TV_series)" title="Weeds (TV series)">Weeds</a></i>, and in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Mean_Girls_2" title="Mean Girls 2">Mean Girls 2</a></i>. Carnegie Mellon was identified as the university &quot;Rat&quot; went to in the science fiction movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Core_(film)" title="The Core (film)" class="mw-redirect">The Core</a>,</i> as well as the university that one of the astronauts attended in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(film)" title="Deep Impact (film)">Deep Impact</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The musical <i><a href="/wiki/Pippin_(musical)" title="Pippin (musical)">Pippin</a></i> was originally conceived by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Schwartz_(composer)" title="Stephen Schwartz (composer)">Stephen Schwartz</a> as a student musical performed by the Scotch'n'Soda student theatre troupe.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Schwartz also collaborated with drama student <a href="/wiki/John-Michael_Tebelak" title="John-Michael Tebelak">John-Michael Tebelak</a> to expand his master's thesis project titled <i><a href="/wiki/Godspell" title="Godspell">Godspell</a></i>, created under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Carra" title="Lawrence Carra">Lawrence Carra</a>, into a musical.</p> 
     <p>While enrolled at Carnegie Mellon, acting students <a href="/wiki/Michael_McKean" title="Michael McKean">Michael McKean</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Lander" title="David Lander">David Lander</a> (class of 1969) created the characters &quot;<i>Lenny &amp; Squiggy</i>&quot;. The pair continued performing the characters in live comedy routines before joining the cast of the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Laverne_and_Shirley" title="Laverne and Shirley" class="mw-redirect">Laverne and Shirley</a></i>.</p> 
     <p>In 2008, Carnegie Mellon professor <a href="/wiki/Randy_Pausch" title="Randy Pausch">Randy Pausch</a>'s &quot;<a href="/wiki/Really_Achieving_Your_Childhood_Dreams" title="Really Achieving Your Childhood Dreams">Last Lecture</a>&quot; became a pop culture phenomenon. Based on a lecture he gave in September 2007 ? shortly after he learned his cancer had metastasized ? his book quickly rose to the top of bestseller lists around the country. Named in <i><a href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" title="Time Magazine" class="mw-redirect">Time Magazine</a></i><span style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s &quot;<a href="/wiki/Time_100" title="Time 100">Time 100</a>&quot; list of influential people, he died in July 2008 from pancreatic cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon also established and administers the <a href="/wiki/Robot_Hall_of_Fame" title="Robot Hall of Fame">Robot Hall of Fame</a> in partnership with the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Science_Center" title="Carnegie Science Center">Carnegie Science Center</a>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_and_divisions">Schools and divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Schools and divisions">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:School_of_design.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/School_of_design.jpg/220px-School_of_design.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/School_of_design.jpg/330px-School_of_design.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/School_of_design.jpg/440px-School_of_design.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:School_of_design.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Margaret Morrison Carnegie Hall, home of the 
        <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Architecture" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Architecture">Carnegie Mellon School of Architecture</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institute_of_Technology" title="Carnegie Institute of Technology">Carnegie Institute of Technology</a></b> includes seven engineering departments: <a href="/wiki/Biomedical_Engineering" title="Biomedical Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Biomedical Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_Engineering" title="Chemical Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Chemical Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_Engineering" title="Civil Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Civil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Engineering" title="Environmental Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Environmental Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_Engineering" title="Electrical Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Electrical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_Engineering" title="Computer Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Computer Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering_and_Public_Policy" title="Engineering and Public Policy">Engineering and Public Policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_Engineering" title="Mechanical Engineering" class="mw-redirect">Mechanical Engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Materials_Science" title="Materials Science" class="mw-redirect">Materials Science</a> and Engineering, and two institutes, the <a href="/wiki/Information_Networking_Institute" title="Information Networking Institute">Information Networking Institute</a> and the Institute for Complex Engineered Systems.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_College_of_Fine_Arts" title="Carnegie Mellon College of Fine Arts">College of Fine Arts</a></b> is one of the oldest colleges of fine arts in the United States, and today it is a federation of schools with professional training programs in the visual and <a href="/wiki/Performing_arts" title="Performing arts">performing arts</a>: Architecture, Art, Design, Drama and Music. The college shares research projects, interdisciplinary centers and educational programs with other units across the university. The college initiated several interdisciplinary program which facilitate graduate student research and enrich their knowledge.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Heinz_College" title="Heinz College">H. John Heinz III College</a></b> offers top-ranked masters degrees in <a href="/wiki/Information_Systems" title="Information Systems" class="mw-redirect">Information Systems</a> and Management, <a href="/wiki/Public_Policy" title="Public Policy" class="mw-redirect">Public Policy</a> and Management, Health Care Policy and Management, Biotechnology and Management, Medical Management, <a href="/wiki/Public_Management" title="Public Management" class="mw-redirect">Public Management</a>, Arts Management, Entertainment Industry Management,Information Technology, and Information Security Policy. It consists of the School of Information Systems &amp; Management and the School of Public Policy &amp; Management. It also offers various PhD and executive education programs.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_College_of_Humanities_and_Social_Sciences" title="Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences">Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences</a></b> is the university's liberal and professional studies college and emphasizes the study of the human condition through rigorous analysis and technology. Departments include <a href="/wiki/English_studies" title="English studies">English</a>, History, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Languages" title="Modern Languages" class="mw-redirect">Modern Languages</a>, Philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">Psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_and_Decision_Sciences" title="Social and Decision Sciences" class="mw-redirect">Social and Decision Sciences</a> and Statistics. The college also offers undergraduate degree programs in <a href="/wiki/Information_systems" title="Information systems">Information systems</a> and Economics.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Mellon_College_of_Science" title="Mellon College of Science">Mellon College of Science</a></b> includes four departments: <a href="/wiki/Biological_Sciences" title="Biological Sciences" class="mw-redirect">Biological Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">Chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Sciences" title="Mathematical Sciences" class="mw-redirect">Mathematical Sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">Physics</a>. In addition, the college is expanding efforts in <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">green chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_biology" title="Computational biology">computational biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_finance" title="Computational finance">computational finance</a>, cosmology, sensor research and biological physics.</li> 
     </ul>
