     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG/170px-Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG/255px-Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG/340px-Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Carnegie-Mellon-University-29_Inside_Gates_Center.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Gates Center is a recent addition to the university's computer science school.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Computer_Science" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Computer Science">School of Computer Science</a></b>: Carnegie Mellon University helped define, and continually redefines, the field of <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>. The School of Computer Science is recognized internationally as one of the top schools for computer science.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a></b> offers undergraduate programs in <a href="/wiki/Business_Administration" title="Business Administration" class="mw-redirect">Business Administration</a> and Economics. Undergraduate Tepper students can choose from an array of tracks including: Finance, Information Technology, <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">Entrepreneurship</a>, Management, <a href="/wiki/Management_consulting" title="Management consulting">Consulting</a>, and Marketing. In addition to choosing a track, undergraduate Tepper students must also choose a minor from one of the other colleges on campus and take a variety of supplemental breadth courses outside of the business program. The Tepper School offers masters degrees in Business Administration (<a href="/wiki/Master_of_Business_Administration" title="Master of Business Administration">MBA</a>) and joint degrees in <a href="/wiki/Computational_Finance" title="Computational Finance" class="mw-redirect">Computational Finance</a> (MSCF) with the Dietrich College of Humanities and Social Sciences, the Mellon College of Science, and the School of Computer Science. In addition, joint degrees are offered with Civil and Environmental Engineering and the Heinz College. The Tepper School also offers doctoral degrees in several areas and presents a number of <a href="/wiki/Executive_education" title="Executive education">executive education</a> programs.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>In addition to the research and academic institutions, the University hosts the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Governor%27s_School_for_the_Sciences" title="Pennsylvania Governor's School for the Sciences">Pennsylvania Governor's School for the Sciences</a>, a state-funded summer program that aims to foster interest in science amongst gifted high school students, and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_Institute_for_Talented_Elementary_and_Secondary_Students&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Carnegie Mellon Institute for Talented Elementary and Secondary Students (page does not exist)">Carnegie Mellon Institute for Talented Elementary and Secondary Students</a> program (C-MITES). The Cyert Center for Early Education is a child care center for Carnegie Mellon faculty and staff, as well as an observational setting for students in child development courses. The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://oli.cmu.edu/">Open Learning Initiative</a> provides free courses online in a variety of fields to students globally.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon University Libraries include Hunt Library, the Roger Sorrells Engineering and Science Library, the <a href="/wiki/Mellon_Institute" title="Mellon Institute" class="mw-redirect">Mellon Institute</a> Library, the Posner Center, and the Qatar Library. Additionally the Libraries manage the <a href="/wiki/Software_Engineering_Institute" title="Software Engineering Institute">Software Engineering Institute</a> Library, and the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Digital_Library" title="Universal Digital Library" class="mw-redirect">Universal Digital Library</a>. The library system includes a number of special collections such as the <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a> Collection, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert A. Simon</a> Collection, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Newell" title="Allen Newell">Allen Newell</a> Collection, the <a href="/wiki/H._John_Heinz_III" title="H. John Heinz III">H. John Heinz III</a> Collection, the Pittsburgh Jewish Newspapers Project, and the Posner Memorial Collection among many others. Carnegie Mellon students and faculty also have access to the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Library_of_Pittsburgh" title="Carnegie Library of Pittsburgh">Carnegie Library of Pittsburgh</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a> libraries through the Oakland Library Consortium. The Hunt Institute for Botanical Documentation (HIBD), a research division of Carnegie Mellon University, and its library collections are located on the top floor of Hunt Library, but are not part of the University Library System.<sup id="cite_ref-test_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon also manages the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Reserve_Officer_Training_Corps" title="Naval Reserve Officer Training Corps" class="mw-redirect">Naval Reserve Officer Training Corps</a> in Pittsburgh on which students throughout Pittsburgh's universities rely. Carnegie Mellon relies on the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a> to provide opportunities in <a href="/wiki/Army_Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Army Reserve Officers' Training Corps">Army Reserve Officers' Training Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Reserve_Officer_Training_Corps" title="Air Force Reserve Officer Training Corps">Air Force Reserve Officer Training Corps</a> to its students.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon University is accredited by the <a href="/wiki/Middle_States_Commission_on_Higher_Education" title="Middle States Commission on Higher Education" class="mw-redirect">Middle States Commission on Higher Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaboration_with_the_University_of_Pittsburgh">Collaboration with the University of Pittsburgh</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Collaboration with the University of Pittsburgh">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon University neighbors the campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>, and in some cases, buildings of the two universities are intermingled. This helps to facilitate myriad academic and research collaborations between the two schools,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> including such projects as the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Supercomputing_Center" title="Pittsburgh Supercomputing Center">Pittsburgh Supercomputing Center</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Life_Sciences_Greenhouse" title="Pittsburgh Life Sciences Greenhouse">Pittsburgh Life Sciences Greenhouse</a>, the Immune Modeling Center, the Center for the Neural Basis of Cognition, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_Cancer_Institute" title="University of Pittsburgh Cancer Institute">University of Pittsburgh Cancer Institute</a>, as well as the National Science Foundation-supported Pittsburgh Science of Learning Center.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Further, the universities also offer multiple dual and joint degree programs such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_School_of_Medicine#MD.2FPhD_Program" title="University of Pittsburgh School of Medicine">Medical Scientist Training Program</a>, the Molecular Biophysics and Structural Biology Graduate Program, the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.compbio.cmu.edu">Joint CMU-Pitt Ph.D. Program in Computational Biology</a>, the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cnbc.cmu.edu">Center for Neural Basis of Cognition</a>, and the Law and Business Administration program. Some professors hold joint professorships between the two schools, and students at each university may take classes at the other (with appropriate approvals).<sup id="cite_ref-PCHE_Cross_Registration_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCHE_Cross_Registration-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> CMU students and faculty also have access to the University of Pittsburgh library system, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Library_of_Pittsburgh" title="Carnegie Library of Pittsburgh">Carnegie Library of Pittsburgh</a>, through the Oakland Library Consortium.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The two universities also co-host academic conferences, such as the 2012 Second Language Research Forum.<sup id="cite_ref-SLRF_2012_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SLRF_2012-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Undergraduate_profile">Undergraduate profile</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Undergraduate profile">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Posner_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Posner_1.jpg/220px-Posner_1.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Posner_1.jpg/330px-Posner_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Posner_1.jpg/440px-Posner_1.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Posner_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Posner Hall, home of the 
        <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>For the incoming class of 2012?2013, the Pittsburgh campus received a record 30,237 unique applicants and admitted 5,802 (19%). 1,409 students enrolled in the class of 2016 (24%).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2012, the most selective undergraduate college was the School of Computer Science, which admitted only 8.2% of total applicants. The largest college, in terms of enrollment, is the Carnegie Institute of Technology with 418 students in the class of 2016, followed by the Dietrich College of Humanities &amp; Social Sciences with 322, and the Mellon College of Science with 230. The smallest college in terms of total undergraduate enrollment is the Tepper School of Business, with 81. Carnegie Mellon enrolls students from all 50 states and the District of Columbia, and 15.6% of the students are citizens of countries other than the United States, representing more than 40 countries. About 96.3% of first-year students enrolled in 2009 returned for their second year, and 72.7% of students in the class of 2010 graduated within four years. Undergraduate tuition is $44,880 and room and board is $11,550.<sup id="cite_ref-fact_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fact-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon graduates 62% of its undergraduate students in <a href="/wiki/STEM_fields" title="STEM fields">science, technology, engineering, and mathematics fields</a>, the 13th highest percentage in the United States amongst national research universities.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg/220px-Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg/330px-Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg/440px-Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Scarab_Lunar_Rover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Scarab_(lunar_rover)" title="Scarab (lunar rover)" class="mw-redirect">Scarab lunar rover</a> is being developed by the RI.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>For the 2006 fiscal year, the University spent $315 million on research. The primary recipients of this funding were the School of Computer Science ($100.3 million), the <a href="/wiki/Software_Engineering_Institute" title="Software Engineering Institute">Software Engineering Institute</a> ($71.7 million), the Carnegie Institute of Technology ($48.5 million), and the Mellon College of Science ($47.7 million). The research money comes largely from federal sources, with federal investment of $277.6 million. The federal agencies that invest the most money are the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a>, which contribute 26% and 23.4% of the total university research budget respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-fact_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fact-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Supercomputing_Center" title="Pittsburgh Supercomputing Center">Pittsburgh Supercomputing Center</a></b> (PSC) is a joint effort between Carnegie Mellon University, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse Electric Company</a>. PSC was founded in 1986 by its two scientific directors, Dr. Ralph Roskies of the University of Pittsburgh and Dr. Michael Levine of Carnegie Mellon University. PSC is a leading partner in the <a href="/wiki/TeraGrid" title="TeraGrid">TeraGrid</a>, the National Science Foundation's cyberinfrastructure program.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Robotics_Institute" title="Robotics Institute">Robotics Institute</a></b> (RI) is a division of the School of Computer Science and considered to be one of the leading centers of robotics research in the world. The Field Robotics Center (FRC) has developed a number of significant robots, including <a href="/wiki/Sandstorm_(vehicle)" title="Sandstorm (vehicle)">Sandstorm</a> and <a href="/wiki/H1ghlander" title="H1ghlander">H1ghlander</a>, which finished second and third in the <a href="/wiki/DARPA_Grand_Challenge" title="DARPA Grand Challenge">DARPA Grand Challenge</a>, and Boss, which won the <a href="/wiki/DARPA_Urban_Challenge" title="DARPA Urban Challenge" class="mw-redirect">DARPA Urban Challenge</a>. The RI is primarily sited at Carnegie Mellon's main campus in Newell-Simon hall.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Software_Engineering_Institute" title="Software Engineering Institute">Software Engineering Institute</a></b> (SEI) is a <a href="/wiki/Federally_funded_research_and_development_center" title="Federally funded research and development center" class="mw-redirect">federally funded research and development center</a> sponsored by the U.S. Department of Defense and operated by Carnegie Mellon University, with offices in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania, USA; Arlington, Virginia, and Frankfurt, Germany. The SEI publishes books on <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> for industry, government and military applications and practices. The organization is known for its <a href="/wiki/Capability_Maturity_Model" title="Capability Maturity Model">Capability Maturity Model</a> <sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup>(CMM) and <a href="/wiki/Capability_Maturity_Model_Integration" title="Capability Maturity Model Integration">Capability Maturity Model Integration</a> (CMMI), which identify essential elements of effective system and software engineering processes and can be used to rate the level of an organization's capability for producing quality systems. The SEI is also the home of <a href="/wiki/CERT_Coordination_Center" title="CERT Coordination Center">CERT/CC</a>, the federally-funded computer security organization. The CERT Program's primary goals are to ensure that appropriate technology and systems management practices are used to resist attacks on networked systems and to limit damage and ensure continuity of critical services subsequent to attacks, accidents, or failures.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Human-Computer_Interaction_Institute" title="Human-Computer Interaction Institute">Human-Computer Interaction Institute</a></b> (HCII) is a division of the School of Computer Science and is considered one of the leading centers of <a href="/wiki/Human-computer_interaction" title="Human-computer interaction" class="mw-redirect">human-computer interaction</a> research, integrating computer science, design, social science, and learning science.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Such interdisciplinary collaboration is the hallmark of research done throughout the university.</p> 
     <p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Language_Technologies_Institute" title="Language Technologies Institute">Language Technologies Institute</a></b> (LTI) is another unit of the School of Computer Science and is famous for being one of the leading research centers in the area of <a href="/wiki/Language_technologies" title="Language technologies" class="mw-redirect">language technologies</a>. Primary research focus of the institute is on <a href="/wiki/Machine_translation" title="Machine translation">machine translation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Speech_synthesis" title="Speech synthesis">speech synthesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_retrieval" title="Information retrieval">information retrieval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parsing" title="Parsing">parsing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Information_extraction" title="Information extraction">information extraction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Until 1996, the institute existed as the Center for Machine Translation that was established in 1986. From 1996 onwards, it started awarding graduate degrees and the name was changed to Language Technologies Institute.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon is also home to the <b><a href="/wiki/Carnegie_School" title="Carnegie School">Carnegie School</a></b> of management and economics. This intellectual school grew out of the <a href="/wiki/Tepper_School_of_Business" title="Tepper School of Business">Tepper School of Business</a> in the 1950s and 1960s and focused on the intersection of behavioralism and management. Several management theories, most notably <a href="/wiki/Bounded_rationality" title="Bounded rationality">bounded rationality</a> and the behavioral <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_the_firm" title="Theory of the firm">theory of the firm</a>, were established by Carnegie School management scientists and economists.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon has made a concerted effort to attract corporate research labs and offices to the Pittsburgh campus. <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disney" title="Disney" class="mw-redirect">Disney</a>, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Inc." title="Bombardier Inc.">Bombardier Inc.</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rand_Corporation" title="Rand Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Rand Corporation</a> have established a presence on or near campus. In collaboration with Intel, Carnegie Mellon has pioneered research into <a href="/wiki/Claytronics" title="Claytronics">claytronics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alumni_and_faculty">Alumni and faculty</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Alumni and faculty">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg/150px-John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg" width="150" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg/225px-John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg/300px-John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:John_f_nash_20061102_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/John_Forbes_Nash" title="John Forbes Nash" class="mw-redirect">John Forbes Nash</a>, subject of 
        <i><a href="/wiki/A_Beautiful_Mind_(book)" title="A Beautiful Mind (book)">A Beautiful Mind</a></i>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Carnegie_Mellon_University_people" title="List of Carnegie Mellon University people">List of Carnegie Mellon University people</a>
     </div> 
     <p>There are more than 92,000 Carnegie Mellon living alumni worldwide. Famous alumni include former <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> CEO and <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_Defense" title="Secretary of Defense" class="mw-redirect">Secretary of Defense</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Erwin_Wilson" title="Charles Erwin Wilson">Charles Erwin Wilson</a>; billionaire <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> investor <a href="/wiki/David_Tepper" title="David Tepper">David Tepper</a>; <a href="/wiki/James_Gosling" title="James Gosling">James Gosling</a>, creator of the <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> programming language; <a href="/wiki/Andy_Bechtolsheim" title="Andy Bechtolsheim">Andy Bechtolsheim</a>, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a>; pop artists <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burton_Morris" title="Burton Morris">Burton Morris</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Mountaineer and Author <a href="/wiki/Aron_Ralston" title="Aron Ralston">Aron Ralston</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Astronauts" title="Astronauts" class="mw-redirect">astronauts</a> <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Mitchell" title="Edgar Mitchell">Edgar Mitchell</a> (of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Judith_Resnik" title="Judith Resnik">Judith Resnik</a>, who perished in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle Challenger</a> disaster. A memorial to Judy Resnik can be found near Porter Hall, sponsored by the engineering fraternity.</p> 
     <p>Overall, Carnegie Mellon is affiliated with eighteen <a href="/wiki/Nobel_laureates" title="Nobel laureates" class="mw-redirect">Nobel laureates</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> twelve <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a> winners, eight <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> recipients, ninety seven <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a> recipients (including ten time recipient <a href="/wiki/Steven_Bochco" title="Steven Bochco">Steven Bochco</a>), and thirty <a href="/wiki/Tony_Award" title="Tony Award">Tony Award</a> recipients (including Andrew Omondi). <a href="/wiki/John_Forbes_Nash" title="John Forbes Nash" class="mw-redirect">John Forbes Nash</a>, a 1948 graduate and winner of the 1994 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Economics" title="Nobel Prize in Economics" class="mw-redirect">Nobel Prize in Economics</a>, was the subject of the book and subsequent film <i><a href="/wiki/A_Beautiful_Mind_(book)" title="A Beautiful Mind (book)">A Beautiful Mind</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Alan_Perlis" title="Alan Perlis">Alan Perlis</a>, a 1943 graduate was a pioneer in programming languages and recipient of the first ever <a href="/wiki/Turing_award" title="Turing award" class="mw-redirect">Turing award</a>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon's student life includes over 225 student organizations, art galleries, and various unique traditions. Student organizations provide social, service, media, academic, spiritual, recreational, sport, religious, political, cultural, and governance opportunities. Carnegie Mellon's campus houses several galleries such as <i>The Frame</i>, a student-devoted gallery, and the <a href="/wiki/Regina_Gouger_Miller_Gallery" title="Regina Gouger Miller Gallery" class="mw-redirect">Regina Gouger Miller Gallery</a>, an art gallery that specializes in contemporary professional artists. The <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Music" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Music">Carnegie Mellon School of Music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_School_of_Drama" title="Carnegie Mellon School of Drama">Carnegie Mellon School of Drama</a> and the student-run theatrical organization <a href="/wiki/Scotch%27n%27Soda" title="Scotch'n'Soda">Scotch'n'Soda</a> provides campus with a variety of world-class performance arts events. The university has a strong Scottish motif inspired by Andrew Carnegie's Scottish heritage, as well as Andrew Mellon's Scots-Irish ancestry. Examples include Scotty, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Terrier" title="Scottish Terrier">Scottish Terrier</a> mascot, <a href="/wiki/The_Tartan" title="The Tartan">The Tartan</a> student newspaper, <a href="/wiki/Skibo_Castle" title="Skibo Castle">Skibo</a> Gymnasium, The <a href="/wiki/Thistle" title="Thistle">Thistle</a> yearbook, and the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9ilidh" title="C&eacute;ilidh">C&eacute;ilidh</a> weekend every fall semester for homecoming.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions">Traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Traditions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ed/The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg/220px-The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ed/The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg/330px-The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ed/The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg/440px-The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:The_Fence_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Fence
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University_traditions" title="Carnegie Mellon University traditions">Carnegie Mellon University traditions</a>
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The Fence</b> ? In the early days of Carnegie Tech, there was a single bridge, which connected Margaret Morrison Women's College with the Carnegie Institute of Technology. The bridge was a meeting place for students. In 1916, the bridge was taken down and the university filled in the area. The administration built a wooden fence as a new meeting place. The students did not understand why anyone would want to meet at a fence. Administration was about to give up and tear it down but that night a fraternity, as a prank, painted the entire fence advertising a fraternity party. Ever since, painting the Fence has been a Carnegie Mellon tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The Fence at Carnegie Mellon lies at the center of campus, in the area known as &quot;the cut.&quot; Students &quot;guard&quot; the fence 24 hours a day, and, as long as two vigils are maintained, no other student may &quot;take&quot; the fence. The fence can then be painted by the group that has it, but only between midnight and 6&nbsp;am. Only hand brushes may be used; the use of spray paint or paint rollers is considered vandalism and results in a fine. The previous paint cannot be stripped, and each new painting adds a new layer. The original wooden fence finally collapsed in the 1990s due to the weight from over 1' of surrounding paint, and was immediately replaced with an identical one manufactured from concrete. Today the fence is considered &quot;the world's most painted object&quot;, by the Guinness Book of World Records.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Spring Carnival</b> ? Usually held in April, Spring Carnival is the biggest event of the school year. In addition to classic carnival attractions, the Spring Carnival features the &quot;Buggy Sweepstakes&quot; and &quot;Booth&quot; (a competition between various organizations to build small, elaborate booths based on a theme chosen each year).</li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg/225px-CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg/338px-CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg/450px-CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:CarnegieMellonBuggy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Two pushers exchange the buggy for 
        <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Delta_Rho" title="Kappa Delta Rho">Kappa Delta Rho</a> on the first hill of Sweepstakes.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Buggy Races</b> ? Buggy, officially called Sweepstakes, is a race around <a href="/wiki/Schenley_Park" title="Schenley Park">Schenley Park</a>. It can be thought of as a relay race with five runners, using the buggy vehicle as the baton. Entrants submit a small, usually torpedo-shaped, vehicle that is pushed uphill and then allowed to roll downhill. The vehicles are unpowered, including the prohibition of such energy-storing devices as flywheels. They are, however, steered by a driver who is usually a petite female student lying prone, arms stretched forward to steer via a turning mechanism. Space is so tight inside the buggies that the drivers usually cannot change position beyond turning their heads.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Mobot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mobot.jpg/225px-Mobot.jpg" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mobot.jpg/338px-Mobot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mobot.jpg/450px-Mobot.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Mobot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A Mobot competing in the annual Mobot challenge
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Mobot</b> ? &quot;Mobot,' a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> for &quot;mobile robot,&quot; is an annual competition at Carnegie Mellon that made its debut in 1994. In this event, robots try (autonomously) to pass through gates, in order, and reach the finish line. There is a white line on the pavement connecting the gates, and the line is normally used to find the gates, though it is not mandated by the rules that the robots follow the line.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Bagpipers</b> ? As one of only a handful of colleges offering a Bachelors degree in <a href="/wiki/Bagpipe" title="Bagpipe" class="mw-redirect">bagpipe</a> music and the only to offer a Masters, Carnegie Mellon University's Pipes and Drums features the sounds of Scottish bagpipes and performs at University events. Director of the Pipes and Drums is champion piper <a href="/w/index.php?title=Andrew_Carlisle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Andrew Carlisle (page does not exist)">Andrew Carlisle</a> from Northern Ireland, a highly decorated solo piper and longtime member of eight times World Pipe Band Champions Field Marshal Montgomery. The ensemble actively competes at Highland Games in Grade Three in the Eastern United States.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>The <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University_Kiltie_Band" title="Carnegie Mellon University Kiltie Band" class="mw-redirect">Kiltie Band</a></b>- Carnegie Mellon's Kiltie Band, dressed in full Scottish regalia including kilts and knee socks, performs during every home football game.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Autographing the Green Room</b> ? Seniors in the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_College_of_Fine_Arts" title="Carnegie Mellon College of Fine Arts">College of Fine Arts</a> sign the Green Room's walls and ceilings before leaving the university. Supposedly, Oscar-winning actress <a href="/wiki/Holly_Hunter" title="Holly Hunter">Holly Hunter</a> broke university tradition by signing the Green Room during her freshman year. With the construction of the Purnell Center in 1998 the School of Drama no longer occupied the old Green Room so this tradition has gone away.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Housing">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Carnegie Mellon offers conventional housing for its students through single-gender, coeducational, and special interest options. Students can choose from standard, prime, or suite-style rooms, efficiencies, one or two bedroom apartments, and houses. There are 20 residential buildings on campus and five off campus in the Oakland area of Pittsburgh.<sup id="cite_ref-CMU_Housing_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMU_Housing-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Most incoming freshmen are assigned to the dedicated freshman residence halls on campus, including: Morewood E-Tower, Residence on Fifth, and the Boss, Donner, Hamerschlag, McGill, Mudge, Scobell, and Stever houses. The upperclassmen who choose to live in university housing do so in the remaining residence halls: Morewood Gardens and West Wing in addition to the Doherty, Fairfax, Margaret Morrison, Neville, Shady Oak, Shirley, and Woodlawn Apartments and the Henderson, Resnik, Roselawn, Spirit, Tech, Webster, and Welch houses.<sup id="cite_ref-CMU_Housing_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMU_Housing-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraternities_and_sororities">Fraternities and sororities</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fraternities and sororities">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities_at_Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Fraternities and sororities at Carnegie Mellon University">Fraternities and sororities at Carnegie Mellon University</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The Greek tradition at Carnegie Mellon University began over 100 years ago with the founding of the first fraternity on campus, <a href="/wiki/Theta_Xi" title="Theta Xi">Theta Xi</a>, in 1912. The Panhellenic sorority community was founded in 1945, by <a href="/wiki/Chi_Omega" title="Chi Omega">Chi Omega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Delta_Delta" title="Delta Delta Delta">Delta Delta Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Gamma" title="Delta Gamma">Delta Gamma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Alpha_Theta" title="Kappa Alpha Theta">Kappa Alpha Theta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Kappa_Gamma" title="Kappa Kappa Gamma">Kappa Kappa Gamma</a>. The Chi Omega chapter at Carnegie Mellon transformed into an independent sorority, Zeta Psi Sigma, and has since become <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Chi_Omega" title="Alpha Chi Omega">Alpha Chi Omega</a>. There are two Asian-interest sororities ? <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Phi_Lambda" title="Kappa Phi Lambda">Kappa Phi Lambda</a> chapter and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Kappa_Delta_Phi" title="Alpha Kappa Delta Phi">alpha Kappa Delta Phi</a> colony.</p> 
     <p>Currently, Carnegie Mellon University has twelve active Fraternities: <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Epsilon_Pi" title="Alpha Epsilon Pi">Alpha Epsilon Pi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Tau_Delta" title="Delta Tau Delta">Delta Tau Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Sigma" title="Kappa Sigma">Kappa Sigma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pi_Kappa_Alpha" title="Pi Kappa Alpha">Pi Kappa Alpha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Alpha_Epsilon" title="Sigma Alpha Epsilon">Sigma Alpha Epsilon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Chi" title="Sigma Chi">Sigma Chi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Nu" title="Sigma Nu">Sigma Nu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Phi_Epsilon" title="Sigma Phi Epsilon">Sigma Phi Epsilon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Tau_Gamma" title="Sigma Tau Gamma">Sigma Tau Gamma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Upsilon" title="Delta Upsilon">Delta Upsilon</a> (colony), <a href="/wiki/Phi_Delta_Theta" title="Phi Delta Theta">Phi Delta Theta</a> (colony), and <a href="/wiki/Lambda_Phi_Epsilon" title="Lambda Phi Epsilon">Lambda Phi Epsilon</a>.</p> 
     <p>In addition to participating in campus traditions such as Buggy and Booth, the fraternities and sororities hold an annual fundraiser called Greek Sing, one of the largest Greek events of the year. Each year, the organizations vote on a cause to support and raise money through ticket sales, ad sales, corporate sponsorships and donations. Each organization performs a 13-minute long original show or a rendition of a popular show. In Spring 2010, Greek Sing raised over $42,000 for St. Jude Children's Research</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>The Carnegie Mellon Tartans were a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/University_Athletic_Association" title="University Athletic Association">University Athletic Association</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Division_III_(NCAA)" title="Division III (NCAA)">NCAA Division III</a>. Prior to World War II Carnegie Mellon (as Carnegie Tech) played with <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">NCAA Division I</a> teams and in 1939 the Tartan football team earned a trip to the NCAA National Championship at the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Bowl" title="Sugar Bowl">Sugar Bowl</a>. That same year, Robert Doherty, university president at the time, banned the football team from competing in postseason bowl games. In 1936 the Carnegie Tech riflery team won the national intercollegiate championship.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Currently, varsity teams are fielded in basketball, track, cross country, football, golf, soccer, swimming, volleyball, tennis, and cheerleading. In addition, club teams exist in ultimate frisbee,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">rowing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_Rugby_Football_Club&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Carnegie Mellon Rugby Football Club (page does not exist)">rugby</a>, lacrosse, hockey,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> baseball,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> softball, skiing &amp; snowboarding, water polo,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and cycling.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Carnegie Mellon Athletics runs a comprehensive and popular intramural system, maintains facilities (primarily Skibo Gymnasium, University Center, and Gesling Stadium), and offers courses to students in fitness and sports. Carnegie Mellon's primary athletic rivals are fellow <a href="/wiki/University_Athletic_Association" title="University Athletic Association">UAA</a> schools <a href="/wiki/Case_Western_Reserve_University" title="Case Western Reserve University">Case Western Reserve University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis" title="Washington University in St. Louis">Washington University in St. Louis</a>; the Tartans have an especially intense rivalry with the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis_football" title="Washington University in St. Louis football" class="mw-redirect">latter's football team</a>.</p> 
     <div class="center"> 
      <div class="thumb tnone"> 
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:772px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gesling.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gesling.jpg/770px-Gesling.jpg" width="770" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gesling.jpg/1155px-Gesling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gesling.jpg/1540px-Gesling.jpg 2x" /></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption"> 
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gesling.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
         </div> Carnegie Mellon's Gesling Stadium taken during the 2009 opening game against Ohio Wesleyan University.
        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Football">Football</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Football">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>On November 28, 1926, the 6?2 Carnegie Tech football team shutout <a href="/wiki/Knute_Rockne" title="Knute Rockne">Knute Rockne</a>'s undefeated <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Fighting_Irish_football" title="Notre Dame Fighting Irish football">Notre Dame Fighting Irish</a> 19?0 at <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Field" title="Forbes Field">Forbes Field</a>. It would be the only loss for the Irish all season and only the second time they allowed a touchdown that season.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The game was ranked the fourth-greatest upset in <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a> history by <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bowl_game_and_AP_rankings">Bowl game and AP rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bowl game and AP rankings">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In the 1930s Carnegie Tech (as it was known then) was among the top football programs in the country. In 1938 and 1939 the team achieved national rankings in the AP Poll. Carnegie Tech earned a January 1 bowl game date following their 1938 campaign in the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Bowl" title="Sugar Bowl">Sugar Bowl</a> losing 15?7 to <a href="/wiki/1938_TCU_Horned_Frogs_football_team" title="1938 TCU Horned Frogs football team">TCU</a>.</p> 
     <p>Carnegie Tech's AP ranking history includes:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>October 17, 1938 #13</li> 
      <li>October 24, 1938 #16</li> 
      <li>October 31, 1938 #19</li> 
      <li>November 7, 1938 #6</li> 
      <li>November 14, 1938 #6</li> 
      <li>November 21, 1938 #7</li> 
      <li>November 28, 1938 #6</li> 
      <li>December 5, 1938 #6 FINAL</li> 
      <li>October 16, 1939 #15</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_resurgence">Decline and resurgence</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Decline and resurgence">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The team lost 26 straight games from 1942 through 1948 (the 1944 and 1945 seasons were cancelled due to <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>). In the last game of the 1948 season, the team beat <a href="/wiki/Grove_City_College" title="Grove City College">Grove City</a>, 7-0, on a 51-yard touchdown run by freshman halfback John Luchok. The team improved over the next six years, culminating in the first undefeated season in school history in 1954. That team was led by quarterback Guy Carricato, halfback Eddy Miller and end Chuck Luchok, John Luchok's younger brother.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_achievements">Modern achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern achievements">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In 2006, the varsity football team was offered a bid to the NCAA Division III playoffs, and became one of the first teams in school history (the first team to win a Division III playoff game was in 1977, when Carnegie Mellon beat Dayton) and University Athletic Association (<a href="/wiki/University_Athletic_Association" title="University Athletic Association">UAA</a>) conference history to win an NCAA playoff game with a 21?0 shutout of <a href="/wiki/Millsaps_College" title="Millsaps College">Millsaps College</a> of the SCAC conference.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition to winning a playoff game, several team members were elected to the All American and All Region Squads. The 2006 team won more games in a single season than any other team in school history. The current coach is <a href="/wiki/Rich_Lackner" title="Rich Lackner">Rich Lackner</a>, who is also a graduate of Carnegie Mellon and who has been the head coach since 1986.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Track_and_cross_country">Track and cross country</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Track and cross country">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In recent years, the varsity track and <a href="/wiki/Cross_country_running" title="Cross country running">cross country</a> programs have seen outstanding success on the <a href="/wiki/Division_III_(NCAA)" title="Division III (NCAA)">Division III</a> national level. The men's cross country team has finished in the top 15 in the nation each of the last three years, and has boasted several individual <a href="/wiki/All-America" title="All-America">All-Americans</a>. The men's track team has also boasted several individual All-Americans spanning sprinting, distance, and field disciplines. Recent All-Americans from the track team are Brian Harvey (2007?2009), Davey Quinn (2007), Nik Bonaddio (2004, 2005), Mark Davis (2004, 2005), Russel Verbofsky (2004, 2005) and Kiley Williams (2005).</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volleyball">Volleyball</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Carnegie_Mellon_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Volleyball">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>With much of the team's support, Lauren Schmidt received the NCAA Pennsylvania Woman of the Year award (2003), was a two-time All-American (2001 and 2002), a four-time All-University Athletic Association selection (1999?2002), and the conference's Player of the Year (2001).<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
